THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL.Sweet Girl Graduates.
Acting under orders from Captain of De
tectives Martin, the detectives went forth
yesterday in quest of bunko men, so that
the visiting Shriners would not be tempt
ed.by the glitter of gold bricks or the pos
session of high hands drawn from â¢ cold
decks of cards. As a result of the sleuths'
search, seven of the fraternity spent the
night at the Central station and will re
main there until the visitors have depart
ed and the town settles back into ordi
nary conditions. None of the men were
charged with any crime, but will be de
tained on the "small" book.

Graduates Entertain Teachers.
Large crowds of Shriners and their
friends viewed the sights in Chinatown
last night. Every available guide was
â¢pressed into service and many were
obliged to find their way about without di
rection' from any one acquainted with the
territory. . During the night It is esti
mated that at least 5000 strangers found
interest in the doings of Little China and
satisfied their curiosity as to things much
written about concerning the colony of
heathens. The Josshouses were thronged
and the "dope" fiends, who smoke for the
amusement of visitors, carried away their
dimes and nickels in sacks. The restau
rants were well patronized, and chop suey
and other Chinese delicacies were, much
in demand. A Chinese festival in progress
was the occasion for brilliant decorations
and much racket, all of which were heart
ily enjoyed.'

Ing up Market street, all arrayed in their
the ferry or railroad depot they
were hospitably welcomed by the mem
bers "of the - reception committee who
awaited them. The last party to arrive
was Moolah Temple, from St. Louis, that
special coming in at 5:30 p. m. *v
Each delegation was met at the ferry by
the local reception committee and the Is
lam Arab patrol, forty-one in number, all
In smart uniforms and carrying their so
ciety emblem, the star and crescent. The

THE Shriners* parade tbjs
evening promises to furnish a
scene of picturesque beauty such
as has not been witnessed in
this city since the memorable
procession of the Native Sons
* during the semi-centennial cel
ebration. Grand Marshal Reuben P.
Hurlbut has estimated that there will be
from eight to ten thousand Shriners in
line. The number, however, will be the
least important' factor, as the parade
throughout Its length will be a gorgeous
spectacle. The marching caravans, with
each individual arrayed in Arabic cos
tume, will impart to the procession the
flavor of an oriental pageant.
The course of the parade along Market
street will be beneath a veritable flood of
radiance. The thousands of Incandescent
lights which have been strung at regular
intervals across the street will all be .
sparkling, and many of the public build
ings will be brilllanty iluminated to light
the marching hosts on their way.
The parade will be divided into
thirteen divisions and each will be
hetaded by a band. Drum corps
and bands will also be Interspersed
through all the divisions so that
there will be music enough for alL The
parade will form at 7 o'clock sharp
and the start will be made at 8*
o'clock. The grand marshal promises that
there will be no delay in getting off
prompOy on time. The line of march will
be from Market and Ninth streets down
Market to Montgomery, countermarching
to Ninth street. It is expected that the
parade will be doubled along its entire
length and that when the head reaches
Ninth street on the countermarch the rear
division will just be commencing to move.
On reaching Ninth street those mounted
will dismount and those in carriages will
alight and the entire parade will pro
ceed to Mechanics' Pavilion, where it will
be reviewed by Imperial Potentate Philip
G. Shaffer.
FEATURES OF THE PARADE.
One of the special features and in all
probability the one which will excite the
most interest will be the maneuvers of
the Arab patrols. There will be six pa
trols In ail and each rider will be dressed
tn Arabic costume. Thirty Shriners
mounted on white horses magnificently
caparisoned will comprise the patrol of
the local temple. Islam Temple enter
tains a feeling of great pride for this pa
trol, and well it may, as $1200 has been ex
pended in properly equipping horses and
riders. It was this patrol that under the
leadership of Reuben P. Hurlbut, the
grand marshal of the present parade, won
the prize at Louisville.
All along the line of march the patrols
will execute maneuvers. The horses will
not be allowed for a moment to walk, but
will be kept continually running, wheel
ing and prancing. Each patrol will be al
lowed to eo through Its maneuvers but
the movements will not be allowed to de
lay the parade. The horses have been
Ehod with rubber shoes, so as not to slip
on the asphaltum.
There will be floats representing scenes
In various countries. There will be four
wagon trains, each drawn by eight voke
of oxen. The trains will be under* the
supervision of H. F. Burns and will be
filled with pioneers. These wagons drawn
by the sturdy oxen are intended to be
emblematic of the trains that were used
by the pioneers In crossing the great
prairie in the early days of California,
and they may also be considered as em
blematic of the caravans drawn by the
faithful camels and used by the Arabs of
the desert. Several of the temples will
Lave camels in line, and Al Kader of Or
egon will be there with a whole drove of
the stately beasts.
THE MORNING PARADE.
There will also b* a parade In the morn
ing, but this event is not intended so
much for the eyes of spectators as It 1*
to furnish a proper escort for the Im
perial Council at .Golden Gate Hall. Reu
ben P. Hurlbut will act as grand marshal,
end he will be assisted by the same aids
ee will serve at the grand parade in the
evening.
The morning parade will form on New
Montgomery street, with its right resting
en Market eJtreet. It will commence to
move at 9 o'clock. The line of march will
fce up Montgomery street to California, to
Kearny to Market, to Stockton to Post,
to Powell to Sutter and thence to Golden
GatÂ© H*1L
The parade will be> composed of 1000
men, 100 of whom will be mounted and
attired in white. Appropriate music will
be furnished, and, although the parade is
not really intended as a display for the
public, nevertheless it will prove a very
pretty epectade.
For the parade in the evening the grand
marshal has issued the following direc
tions:
Formation from Tenth to Ninth street,
column of threes; at Ninth street, column of
sixes, open order. Thie formation to be main
tained until arrival at Ninth street on counter
march. Form column of threes and enter Pa
vilion, passing review in column of sixes.

MENU CARD A. WORK OF ART.
Committee on Banquet Preparing a
Splendid Programme for Sat
urday Night.
The souvenlr^nenu card that will be is
sued at the grand banquet in the Me
chanics' Pavilion on Saturday, the last
night of Shriner week. Is a work of art.
It is being prepared by Noble George
Spaulding, a member of Islam Temple
and one of the executive body of the cele
bration committee. The cover Is leather,
In which there is a long, oblong opening.
Around the upper part of this opening are
photographs of Arabic figures. Under the
opening is a piece of light-blue cardboard,
at the top of which is a fleeting cloud of
gold and below are three golden poppies,
emblematic of the State. On the pages
within are the menu, the* music for the
evening and the list of toasts; after the
election there will be added the names of
the new officers of the Imperial Council,
as well as those of the executive^ commit
tee of Islam Temple.

to be rendered during the banquet:

It Is doubtful if a better illumination
has ever been seen here. Most of the
large buildings were covered with incan
descent lights of all colors. The ferry
building was a magnificent spectacle.
Every line of the structure had bÂ«en fol

One of the prettiest sights was the Claus
Spreckels building, with its massive sides

white lights. The City Hall presented
another grand display of what can be ac
complished in the way of decorating with
Incandescent lamps.

"Whitewash" Affidavit Is Secured From Employe
Til /fF^^^ documents are being prepared at San Quentin prison at the dictation of the officials. On Sunday evening last Commissary Foley, Assistant Commissary Gilardin and Steward Pretzel were required to sign a
/ 11/ if e P os '^ on to the effect that they had never furnished The Call with any information as to the affairs of San Quentin, and that they had no knowledge of any fraud, forgery or falsification of records in the com-
The deposition was prepared by Dan Kevane, former "outside law partner" of Governor Gage in Los Angeles. A prison vehicle was sent to San Rafael and the stenographer of the prison, C. M. Reynolds,
who is also a Notary Public, was brought in haste from his home to the penitentiary. When Reynolds arrived and Dan. Kevane 'had talked with him, Commissary Foley, Assistant Commissary Gilardin and Steward Pretzel
were sent for. They were required to sign the deposition and swear to the truth of the document. Without hesitation Foley,. Gilardin and Pretzel affixed their signatures to the deposition, and Reynolds, the prison stenog
rapher and Notary Public, certified to their acts. Foley, Gilardin and Pretzel are employes of Warden Aguirre and they surmised the fate in store for them if they did not do as required by Dan Kevane, the man Friday of
Governor Gage. A
Stenographer and Rotary Public Reynolds was seen last evening at His home in San Rafael. He admitted that he had been warned to < say nothing to any. one of what is transpiring in San Quentin prison. He
would not deny that he had certified to the depositions of Foley, Gilardin and Pretzel, but when informed that The Call possessed all the details of the depositions and the certification of the signatures Reynolds replied:
"You seem to know all about the affair, so what is the use of asking me anything, about it?" Reynolds then entered his house and refused to talk further.
Prisoners have been forced to forge bills and falsify records at San Quentin prison by order of their superiors. The., latest move is to compel employes to sign depositions to bolster up the "star chamber investiga
tion" of Governor Gage and his cronies. / . :^ : â¢

ole the board will require tho written

without violating the law. It shaU
be the duty of such "officers to obtain
all the information possible of the

Governor Gage and the Board
of Prison Directors cannot plead
that- they were ignorant of the
rules and regulations ; governing
the parole of convicts. The rules
and regulations , were formulated
by the Board of Directors acting
under the authority of an act of
the Legislature, and Governor
'Gage has often had occasion to
consult those rules. Governor
Gage simply asked the Board of
Prison Directors to parole Mah
Noon, and the request was grant
ed. In no instance did the Gov
ernor or the, Directors comply
with the rules and regulations of
the parole law. Rule 2 of the pa
role regulations provides as fol
lows :

Â¦ George E. White's was a remarkable
career. He was born in Lewis County,
Virginia, on August 17, 1832, and was re
lated through his mother to the celebrated
Jackson family. General Stonewall Jack
son having been his cousin. In April, 1843,
he started with some relatives across the
plains to California, traveling with ox
teams and arriving at Lawson's ranch on
Deer Creek in September. For a -time he
followed mining in Shasta County, but,
growing ill, he returned to the Sacra
mento Valley. After trying various occu
pations in different- places he finally, in
May, 1S54, crossed the mountains from
Stony Creek, in company with five others,
bent upon prospecting tor gold, and
chanced to enter Round Valley. He found
that a party headed by one Kelsey had
been there- before him, but had gone.
.-White located a ranch of about 1000
acres and in 1857 went to reside there per
manently. The first house in Round Val
ley was a cabin on his land.- Continuing
to acquire land he finally became owner
of 35,000 acres in Mendoclno County and
30,000 acres in Trinity County, with. 30,000
sheep and thousands of cattle.
-In 1867 White returned to Virginia, and
in May, .1863, married Miss Alice Fettey.
He brought his wife to Round Valley,
where, in 1873, she died of consumption.
In 1878 he erected on his broad domain
the most palatial residence at that time
north of San Francisco. Later he mar
ried Miss Frankie White; the beautiful
sister of Clarence White, the man who
afterward killed Wylackie John: The cat
tle king and his young.wlfa did not live
happily long, and a sensational divorce
suit followed, in which Mrs. White was
awarded $100,000. â¢ .- .

to l.he White House and frankly telllns
the President it was high time to investi
gate the interference and Influence of Fed
eral office-holders in Ohio State politics.
This in addition to. the representations al

sentative Burton, whose friend was. de
feated for chairman of the Republican
County committee by a quaitet cf Govtrn
mer.t officials in Cleveland, has increaseu
the inteiest in what now promises .to be
come an exciting episode m national Re
publican politics, senator Foraker's at
titude in the matter is not surprising, as
he is now underneath in the political skir
mish in the BucKeye State. President
Roosevelt listened with considerable sur
prise to Foraker's plain statements but
promised nothing in the way of an in
vestigation.
Another incident of the day was the
conference between Senator Hanna and
Civil Service Commissioner James R. Gar
field. GarKeld is expected to go to Cleve
land this week and may carry instruc
tions to make an inquiry into political af
fairs there. Senator Foraker takes the
ground that Collector Leach, Marshal
Chandler, Deputy Marshal Hopkins and
Postmaster Dewstoe; have been guilty of
a violation of the civil service regula
tions and especially of a recent order of
the President. In this he takes direct Is
sue with Senator Hanna and thus the
President again tiecomes involved in the
Hanna-Foraker factional light. One out
coma of the affair may be the candidacy
of Burton for the senatorship against Sen
ator Hanna with the support of the For
aker faction. If an investigation, which
seems to be inevitable, is ordered and re
sults in tho censure or removal of the
Cleveland officials it will result in a
breach between the President and. Senator
Hanna. which all the machine politicians
in Washington admit will be disastrous to
the party. If President Roosevelt con
sider the charges against these men are
of a serious character, he will be com
pelled to act or be charged with shielding

ers cf the Chairman of the Republican

sections 7 and 8 of the law . under
which the labor bureau is operated
charged the commissioner to investigate
and renort on all labor disputes.

ditions, as a patriotic citizen President

cdvise and suggest, and the efficial inves

John F. Moody was a rich stage line
owner of- that mountain town and when
he died it was found that he had appoint
ed Richardson to serve as one of his exec
utors, the other being his son, John F.
Moody Jr.
. Both Moody and Richardson were heav
ily interested in the Tahoe Ice Company,
the former owning 1000 shares of stock
and Richardson 800 shares. As executor,
Richardson sold the Moody shares, osten
sibly, for $15,000 and his action was ap
proved by the Superior Court of Nevada
County.
Now comes the widow of the deceased,
.Hattie E. Moody; the other executor un
der the will, John F. Moody Jr., and Les
ter Brown Moody, who sue to compel
Richardson to show to the court . what
amount in excess of $15,000 he received for
Moody's stock. The allegation is made
that he sold the stock for $30,000 and con
verted $15,000 of. the proceeds to his own
use.
It is probable the suit will be trans
ferred to Nevada County for trial.

DOVER. Del., June 9.-It was reported
here to-day that the authorities of the San
Francisco criminal courts are not willing
to grant a remuneration to the Delaware
witnesses needed to rc-try Mrs. Coidelia
Botkin. accused of the murder of Mrs
John P. Dunning of Dover by means of
poisoned candy, except a reasonable per
diem and actual expenses. This, it is
contended, the witnesses are unwilling to
accept for the enforced stay of seven
weeks or more from their-homes and the
perils of a transcontinental trip, and un
less an agreement can be reached it is
feared Mrs. Botkin may go free
Within the past fortnight. Judge Cook
the trial Judge for the people of Cali
fornia, was heard to say that while crim
inals must be brought to justice there
even at great cost, there must, however'
be some limit to the expense of getting
witnesses. He was referring to the state
ment that the Delaware witnesses par
ticularly the professional men and busi
ness men, proposed to be well paid be
fore they . should again turn over their
business affairs to others. The physi
cians particularly say that they cannot
go under any other considerations, and
do not desire to go at all, leaving their
practice and particular patients to the
care of .others. Delawareans who have
lately been in San- Francisco declare that
the feeling. is still high against Mrs. Bot
kin and that any jury would convict her
of the murder, were they confronted with
the same strong' case that was made
against her before.
Delaware Witnesses Against Alleged
Poisoner Do Not Care to Come
Out Here.
MRS. CORDELIA BOTKIN"
MAY NOT BE PROSECUTED
BERLIN, June 9.â King Albert of Sax
ony, who has been ill for some time, was
unconscious for a part of last night. His
Majesty was surrounded by his family,
who momentarily expected the end, and
he had received the last sacraments. This
morning, however, his Majesty sat up and
had breakfast, smoked a cigar and in
sisted upon disposing of some state busi
ness. This exhibition of vitality on the
part of the King has quite startled his
physicians who, however, entertain the
gravest fears for the next relapse.
The only member of King Albert's fam
ily who is now absent from his Majesty's
bedside is his son Max, who is a priest
and a professor of theology in a Catholic
institution at Sable Island.
Dying Monarch Sits Up in Bed and
Disposes of Some State
Business.

CHICAGO, June 9.â Nine men and one
woman were killed and about thirty per
eons injured in a flre which this afternoon
destroyed the . sanitarium conducted by
the Bt. Luke's Society at Wabash avenue
and Twenty-first street. The society oc
cupied the building which was long known
as the Hotel Woodruff and for a brief
period as the Hotel Lancaster. The
greater portion of the patients received in
the institution were those seeking cure
from the drink habit and those who were
addicted to the use of drugs. - When the
fire broke out there were on the fifth floor
a number of patients suffering from de
lirium tremens and ' some who were de
ranged by drugs. Some of these were
strapped to their beds and it was found
Impossible to save them, so' rapidly did
the flre spread through the building.

JUMPED FROM WINDOWS.
The fire originated In the basement of
the building and spread rapidly to the
upper stories through the elevator shaft
Before the occupants of the building could
be warned the flames had been carried
to the roof, had eaten through it and
were leaping high in the air.
As the cry of fire rang through the
building patients sprang from their beds,
and before they could be prevented sev
ei-al had jumped from the windows to the
pavement. The fire department was on
the scene within a few minutes, and as
the windows were filled with people
shrieking for help the firemen devoted
their tirst efforts to save the lives andvkl
lowed the fire to burn. While this was
the means of saving a large number of
-people who were carried down ladders by
the firemen it gave the fire such headway
that there was almost no chance for those
on the upper floors of the building to
make their escape, and 6uch of them as
were not suffocated were killed or badly
Injured by -leaping from the windows.
KENT'S AWFUI* DEATH.
Alderman Kent, one of the dead, was in
a room on the fifth floor with his atten
dant, A. W. Wattells. The Alderman, who
has been totally blind for many years,
was in a straitiacket and his hands
were manacled to a belt that passed
around his waist. When the alarm of flre
was sounded Wattells ran to investigate.
He found the elevator shaft a mass of
flames and ran back to help the Alder
man, who was blind and unable to do
anything with his hands, and who was
almost mad with fear and was shouting
like an Insane person. He had groped h!s
way to the door and by the time his at
tendant had reached him had fallen to
the floor overcome with smoke. Wattells
seized him and tried to drag him down the
hall to a place of safety, but Kent had
become so crazed that It was almost im
possible to do anything- with him. Wat
tells was finally compelled to run for his
life, as the flames were scorching his
clothing. He ran to a window on the
6outh side of the building, across which
were iron bars. Wattells managed to tear
two or three of these from their fasten
ings, and, with two other men who haÂ«l
followed him, climbed out on the sill.
They were seen by the firemen,
woo called to them to remain
vhtre they were and they would
MTe them. A ladder was run up
and the two men who were with Wattells
were taken down. He became crazed with
excitement and not waiting for the return
of the firemen sprang for a net which
some men were holding on the Bid walk be
neath. He fell partly ln it and partly on
the sidewalk and sustained injuries which
probably will cause his death.
Kent was left lying on the floor of his
room and evidently died of suffocation.
His body was badly burned after death.
Three hours after the outbreak of the
flre the body was found on the floor of his
room, one leg burned to a crisp and the
head burned off. He was Identified by
means of his clothing.

j PORTLAND. Ore., June 9.â Harry
Tracy -'/was arrested in Portland on the
evening- of February 6, 1899, after an ex
citing fight with Detective David Wiener.
Tracy, who was wanted for burglary,
was walking along the- "street when De
tective Weiner met him. The two walked
together and when they reached the cor
ner of Fourth and Market streets, Tracy,
seeing the southbound Northern Pacific
train pulling up Fourth street, said: â¢
"I guess I'll take this train. So long." ;
"I guess you won't," said the detective,
â¢ drawing his revolver. â¢ .... : Â¦ '
Tracy fired two shots at the detective,
who returned the fire. Tracy jumped into
the engine and pointed his revolver at the
head of the engineer, who speedily held
up his hands. Tracy ordered- him to go
ahead at full speed, but the conductor
stopped the train. ; Seeing- that his plan
had been frustrated, Tracy jumped from
the engine and started to run. The neigh
borhood had been thoroughly aroused and
after a half-dozen shots had been fired
at the fugitive, one -of which took effect
behind his ear, he- was lodged in jail.
A month afterward, while being taken
from jail to -the court room,. Tracy sud
denly drew a revolver and exchanged
several shots with the jailer, but was
finally overpowered.

HERMOSILLO, Mexico, June 8.â To W.
Loalza & Co.. 216 Sansome street, San Fran
cisco: Yesterday noon State and Federal troops
under Torres attacked and completely crushed
Yaquis In their stronghold in Mazatan Moun
tain.. DetaiU later. & CO. Â¦
The private dispatch from
Hermosillo to a firm in this city confirms
The Call's dispatches regarding the de
struction of the Yaqui revolutionary force
in Sonora: .
It is believed that this defeat means an
end to Yaqui resistance to the sovereignty
of Mexico for all time. The flower of the
Yaaul fighting force has been destroyed,
and those who escaped will be hunted re
lentlessly by the Mexican regulars. | Thus
has passed out of existence as a warlike
factor a tribe whose bravery and indomit
able perseverance against accepting the
fate of a conquered people i has made , it
distinctive- among the aborigines south of
the American boundary. -- -<^ , .-
PASSING OF BBAVE TBIBE.
-Reports from the mountain strongholds
of the Yaquls gave their. number as 2000,
but it is said' that in yesterday's battle
they marshaled their entire strength and
had only about - 600 / braves opposed to
tl Torres 1C f ound^the ' Yaquis intrenched
twenty miles northeast of Mineas Prietas.
Contrary to their usual tactics, the braves
welcomed the attack, instead of resorting
to their ordinary naode^ of- re.^de
pending upon the strength of their earth
works and the advantage of position to
W The h batt a ie' lasted several hours. In the
end the Mexicans scaled the fortifications,
where the Yaquis made a last stand In a
hand-to-hand encounter. When at last the
survivors gave up the unequal struggle
and beat a precipitate retreat, hundreds of
their number lay dead or dying in the
trenches or were prisoners in the hands
of the Mexicans.
YAQUIS ABE OUTNUMBEBED.
' TUCSON, Arfiz., June 9.â The Yaqui re
bellion in the Mexican State of- Sonora
has- been crushed out. In. a battle fought
wjtbin fifteen miles of Hermosillo yester
day afternoon scores of the Indians were
slain and the remnants of the band that
were not wounded or captured were
driven in confusion to mountain* fast
nesses, where they are hiding from their
Mexican conquero'rs. The Mexican loss in
killed in the. combat is given in private
advices as forty-eight. The number, of
the wounded has not been learned , here,
nor has the exactness of the Yaquis*. the
dispatches . only announcing that the In
dian force was . completely crushed and
its survivors scattered. o Â¦Â¦
The Mexican army, numbering 2500 men.
under the command of General Torres,
the veteran Yaqui fighter, moved out of
Iiermosillo severaldays ago, after the ar
rival of the reinforcements which Torres
had asked for, and marched toward the
Mazatan Mountains, where the Indiana
had gathered in force. It was the inten
tion of General Torres to engage the rev
olutionists in open battle, if P<>ss Ib Je. and
end the rebellion with one telling blow.

There ought, to-be "some way for statesmen to set
tle aaebjate of this kind that has been prolonged for
years. The Spooner bill offers a way. It may not be
the best bill that is conceivable, but it is a great deal
better than endless wrangling. It provides a means
of getting something done. It means business.
The question ought to be settled at once. Both the
great political parties of the country have repeatedly
declared in favor of a canal, and about every import
ant 'commercial or industrial organization in the
Union has urged prompt action. Examination after
examination has been made of the various routes and
all the facts that are obtainable are now in the posses
sion of Congress and the nation. ' It is clear there will
never be a unanimous agreement as to the route.
The danger is that between the advocates of the rival
enterprises Congress may be deadlocked for years to
come. In this Congress the , House of Representa
tives has passed a bill providing for the construction
of a canal by the Nicaragua route by an overwhelm
ing majority. That bill i s now threatened with defeat
in the Senate. Should the Senators pass a Panama
bill, it would probably; be defeated in the House. Thus
the work would be blocked. It would be a victory for
those who wish no canal at all.
In. this situation, Boards of Trade, Chambers of
Commerce andj.Other representative bodies should
urge upon Congress the adoption of the plan of pass
ing an isthmian, canal bill, leaving it to the President
"to determine which route is best. Senator Spooner
introduced a bill of that kind soon after the canal bill
was sent up from the House to the Senate.. The issue
therefore would not be a new one; nor would it re
quire any elaborate consideration. It has been fully
discussed in Congress anr 1 by the press and might
readily enough be adopted -t this session.
REPORTS from Washington are ominous of dis
aster to the hopes of those who have expected
the passage of an isthmian canal bill at this
session. The more the. subject is discussed the more
deeply marked appears the division between the ad
vocates of the Nicaragua route and those of the Pan
ama route. The volcanic eruption in the West Indies'
that seemed at one time to lead to a general accep
tance of the Panama line by reason of the menace of
volcanoes along the Nicaragua route has had no.
other effect than that of stirring up counter reports
of (earthquakes at Panama. Each side stands by its
guns and it looks as if there would be a long fight
before the finish. '.â¢' â¢

WASHINGTON, June 9.-The Liberty
Bell arrived here to-day, en route to Phil
adelphia, from the Charleston Exposition
where it was on exhibition. The arrival
of the bell was marked with brief patriot
ic exercises and thousands of people
passed before the car during the day and.
viewed the bell.
During the early part of the session the
naval appropriation , bill was considered.
All of the committee amendments were
disposed of except that relating to the
construction of two additional battleships,
two cruisers and two gunboats.
An interesting discussion' of the isth
mian canal project was made in- the Sen
ate to-day by Harris of Kansas, a member
of the Committee on In teroceanlc Canals,
v,-ho is also an engineer of recognized
ability. His discussion of the technical
details of canal construction was listened
to with profound attention. He strongly
advocated the selection of the Nlcaraguan
route. He maintained that it was entirely
feasible and practicable from an engineer
ing standpoint, and said that some of the
difficulties presented by the Panama route
were insuperable, notably. 1 the'eonstruc
tion'of the Bohlo dam. . .
Mondell of Wyoming and Shafroth of
Colorado supported the minority substi
tute. Bell of Colorado opposed the bill in
toto. No vote was reached. . Â¦ . '_ Â¦ â¢ .
The remainder of the day was devoted
to the bill to transfer certain forest-re
serves from the Interior Department to
the Aglcultural Department, and to au
thorize the creation in such reserves of
game and fish preserves. President Roose
velt in his annual message recommended
such a measure. The minority of the
Public Lands Gommittee offered a Senate
substitute for the bill, a measure which
eliminated that portion of the bill which
transfers the reserves to the Agricultural
Department. Lacey of Iowa, ,Kleberg of
Texas, Perkins of New York and Wil
liams of Missouri spoke in favor of the
bill. ' .'.''.
WASHINGTON, June 9.â The House
passed the bill to protect the President,

and foreign Ministers and Embassadors
and to suppress the teaching of anarchy

As to the platform on which the. banqueters will
build the altar of harmony, it is hardly worth while
to speculate. Cleveland will doubtless assert one
doctrine and Bryan another. It will be left to David
Bennett Hill to adjust the planks and Henry Watter
son will spread over the structure a sufficient'num
ber of flowers of rhetoric to hide the cracks and cover
the points where the misfits meet. Mr. Watterson has
already declared there is no particular need for a plat
form. He has said, "Nothing should go into the plat
form which would tend to drive any one from the
party who supported it in 1896"; and we may infer
that he would also object to anything that would drive
away the Democrats who didn't support the candidate
of that year.-
In a recent issue of. the Courier- Journal Mr. Wat
terson says: "Why should we agitate ourselves over
the platforms of 1896 and 1900, on which we lost two
successive Presidential battles? If it be insisted that
we turn back for a platform, why not that of 1892
on which we won our last Presidential "battle? The
truth is, we should retrace nq footsteps, saddle our
selves with no handicaps, but, turning our backs upon
the past, cur faces to the future and'the foe, we should
gather in the ample- shelter of the old Democratic
The reappearance of the ex- President in the field
will be an interesting fact. ,He has been repudiated by
his party, hooted, hissed and denounced as a political
traitor. Now he is invited to come back and take the
highest. scat at the table. > Naturally there will be a
good deal of curiosity to hear what he is going to say,
and how the other banqueters will receive it. It is
to be hoped Bryan will not shirk the situation. The
country would like to have the pleasure of seeing the
"old" man" and the "boy orator" confronted at the
banquet board> talking harmony and mutual good
will. *
A meeting of such an assemblage of silver men and
gold men, Eastern men' and Western men, Tammany
men and anti-Tammany men, would An itself be a
marked incident in the politics of the day, but the
occasion is to have a brighter luster yet. It is an
nounced that Grover Cleveland has been invited as
the chief guest of the banquet and that he has ac
cepted and will make a speech.
An estimate of the comprehensive nature of the
banquet may be formed from the announcement that
upward of 1500 prominent Democrats have been in
vited to attend the feast. Among those who are ex
pected are: David B. Hill, William J. Bryan, Sen
ator Bacon of Georgia, Mayor Collins of Boston,
John D. Milburn of Buffalo, Richard Olney, the Rev!
Felix Adler, Bourke Cockran, William C. Whitney,
ex-Mayors Grace, Grant, Gilroy, Van Wyck, Hewitt,
Cooper, Edson and Ely, and Lewis Nixon.
ONE more vigorous effort is to. be. made by the
Democratic leaders in New York to formulate
a policy for the party before the Congres
sional campaigns begin. The new movement has
taken the form of a political association, to be known
as "The Tildeii Club." It is to be inaugurated on
the 19th of this month at a banquet, to which prom
inent Democrats of all sections of the country and all
shades of opinion have been invited. A sufficient
number of acceptances have been received to render
it certain that the occasion will be a notable one, and
once more^the hopes of the' reorganizes soar high.

A Pennsylvania professor has been amusing himself
by telling the New York people that a mountain in
the neighborhood of that city was once an active vol
cano and is likely to resume activity at any time. The
New Yorkers are so frightened at the prospect' ot. a'
local Pelee that a good many of them are thinking
of going to Philadelphia, where they can be sure of
a quiet life. ' / ' '", :
FREE UTILITIES.
It is to be hoped that this advance step by the ad
vocates of public ownership will not at all check the
movement for entire reform in public accounting for
a plain and simple system of audit and account 'will
be more necessary under the proposed plan than it
is now. â¢
The new plan of supporting public utilities by tax
ation, and without further charge to the user, admits
everything that is charged against their administra
tion by charge and collection of rates. Granting that
it will economize their administration by io per'cent,
it still remains to be proved that their enjoyment by
the user will be any cheaper than under private admin
istration. The writer above quoted believes that it
will be cheaper, but there are others who believe that
even the essential activities of government, the police
fire and sanitary departments of 'city government'
could be run so much cheaper as a private business
proposition that the saving would constitute a satis
factory business profit
This concedes The Call's premise that public waste
is greater than corporate profit. The plan for can
celing out this element will no doubt now come into
focus in the discussion. Let us get rid of one error
that has already appeared in it. That which is paid
for in any form is not free. We owe this error to the
use of the term "free schools." That term originated
when it was expected that the landed endowment of
our public school system would suffice to support it,
without resort to taxation. That expectation proved
futile, because the lands have been disposed of un
timely and at their minimum value and the fund they
produced has to be supplemented by taxation that
is often complained cf as burdensome. The schools
are paid for, but not by rates exacted of their patrons,
and are free to none. Every renter is a taxpayer and
contributes by rent to support the schools in which
his children are educated, as does every owner of tax
able realty or personalty.
A California writer on the subject mistakenly calls
a water system supported entirely by taxation and
not at all by rates "a free water system," and says of
it: "In the first place it is more economical. The
cost of accounting and collecting is done away with.
I have looked over the reports of cities having so
called municipal systems, and I find that the cost of
keeping the accounts with consumers arid the
collection of water rates amounts in many
cases to over io per cent of the entire op
erating expenses. If this could be dispensed with the
saving would be very material. If this item of ex
pense could be abolished there would be no question
but that public operation would be cheaper than pri
vate operation." â¢
As every investigation has shown that such utilities
are operated at a greater cost than in private hands,
and that public waste more than equals corporate
profit, it is now proposed that the fixing and collec
tion of rates be entirely abandoned. Water, light and
street railroad systems are to be supported directly
by taxation and be free to the use of all without any
further charge. It is claimed that under such a sys
tem it will be positively shown that such utilities will
be furnished cheaper than is possible by private cap
ital.
THE persistent demand for reform in public ac
counting has caused the proponents of public
ownership and operation of public utilities to
take a very important advance step.

ANARCHY BILL
IS PASSED BY
BIG MAJORITY
THE CHIEF topic in railroad circles
yesterday was' the promotion by
President E. H. Harriman of the
Southern. Pacific Company of three
old time officials or the road.
To-day a circular will be sent through
out the "Yellow/Building" notifying the
Southern Patiflc Company employes that
in the future J. "Wilder, who for so many
years has been freight auditor for the
company, with headquarters at Fourth
and Townsend streets, has been promoted
to the position of assistant auditor of the
Southern "Pacific and â¢will remove his of
fices tothe : Wells-Fargo building.
D. W. Horsburgh, who has been for
many years assistant freight auditor of
the Southern Pacific will from to-day 'on
be freight auditor and Henry S. Lincoln
will be his assistant.
The news of the promotion of the three
officials comes as a pleasant surprise to
their friends. Wilder entered the service
of the Central Pacific. way back in 1S68
and was made freight auditor of the road
in 1872, which office he has held up to date.
He is a very popular railroad man and is
deemed strict. yet just.
D. W. Horsburgh, who succeeds Mr
Wilder as freight auditor, has been in the
employ of the Southern Pacific for thirty
years and during that time has been
twenty years assistant freight auditor.
He is deemed one of the most honorable
and painstaking officials in the employ of
the company and his promotion is held
to be well earned.
Henry S. Lincoln won his spurs as con
fidential clerk to J. G. Stubbs, the great
railroad traffic man. Mr. Lincoln was the
right hand man of Mr. Stubbs fromlSSl
to 1901.
The circular which will be issued to-day
It seems almost useless to warn the public against
frauds of this kind. Again and again the newspapers
of the country have had occasion vta'-expose some
bare-faced swindle carried on under the form of some
kind of investment company, and yet the warning
contained in the exposure doe^ little good.- No sooner
,is one rascal captured and imprisoned than another
appears and does a thriving trade. Nor is it always
the poor and the ignorant who are deceived. Some of
the "get rich quick" concerns have found dupes
among all classes of business men. By excluding
their circulars and letters from the mails' the Gov
ernment can. do something to check the evil, and it
is gratifying to know it is to be done; The Govern
ment cannot consent to permit its energies and agen
cies to be made use of for purposes of fraud.
A recent report on the subject says: "The informa
tion already secured was reported by one officer, whose
inspection tour included less than four weeks' work in
a small part of the country. The inspector reports
that there are some thirty firms in Kansas City, be
tvveen_ thirty and forty in Chicago, nearly twenty in
Louisville, eight in Des Moines, and from seven to
twenty in various places throughout the West, from
Lexington, Ky., to Denver, Colo. He said that they
had exhausted the vocabulary of corporation litera
ture."
Most of the dupes of 'the swindlers are working men
and women, have been' induced to make invest
ments in the;various schemes in the fond hope of de
riving a profit. Many of the so-called companies are
new. The profits must be large, for.it is said-they
are springing up rapidly all over the Mississippi Val
ley. Short as has been, their career, however, the
complaints concerning them are already numerous,
and the Postoffice Department has' ample evidence
against them to justify their exclusion from the mails.
WHILE the Government at Washington is in
no sense paternal and does not undertake to
prevent a fool from acting according to his
,folly r and Soon parting himself from his money, yet
} l d 95 s undertake- to prevent fraudulent schemers from
"using, the agencies of the Government in enticing the
unwary to. part with their coin. The Postoffice De
partment has- most to do with 'frauds of this kind,
since it is by means of the mails that the swindlers
most frequently get" into- communication with their
dupes.;" Recently the department has been especially
active' inj.ja'uppressing' fraudulent enterprises' carried
on through the; mails, and according to reports from
Washing|on ; a grea* deal' of good has : bean- accom
plished. 'â¢Â¦'.; ;.::; -.,-;;. ; ; v V "V Â¦- :.:â¢â¢
Of late;the attention: of -the fraud order division of
the office has ; been 'directed to- what are -known W 1
"home corporation:: and '.''bond 1 investment", schemes.
Such 'schemes, it is -said, are ; being worked more ex
tensively and; more ;^ in the West than in
any^otherVart; of- the. country. . An official report sub
mitted to the Postmaster General estimates these
questionable companies have already contracted lia
bilities amounting to- upward of $50,000,060, while
their total available'assets do not equal one-tenth of
that amount. ' '

To our brilliant guests, therefore,. our welcome goes
most heartily. The passwords and the signs of the Mys
tic Shrine can be given them by their fellow Shriners
only, but from all classes of citizens they will receive
the cordial grip of welcome and of hospitality and
know that San Francisco is theirs to command.
Over and above the gratification derived from the
brilliancy of the parades and the general joyousness of
the entertainments that will mark the meeting will
be the satisfaction at having a visit from so many men
of wealth and worth from distant. States'. The Amer
ican custom of holding conventions and assembling in
large masses" has an important effect in developing
a true sense of unity of the people of the whole coun
try and cultivating frie'ndly relations among those
who live in the. farthest separated States. The Shrin-^
ers who have crossed the hot sands from the East
will carry back to. their homes' a memory of some
thing more than the demonstration of the worth of
their order. They will have a fuller realization 6f the
greatness of the republic and the essential oneness of
the vast population of their fellow countrymen. They
will return to add new commendations to the many
pronounced by former travelers upon our State and
our city, and thuls help us to advance until California
holds in the Union; the place to which her natural
gifts entitle her.
, The week of the Mystic Shriners is going to be
something like a grand holiday. The gorgeous uni
forms, the bright colors of banners and flags, the
sparkle of medals and insignia, the blare of trumpets
and the roll cf drums, will enliven the city and give
to our accustomed occidental ways something of the
mystery and the pomp of the marvelous lands where
the crescent and the cross struggled for mastery in the
old days when knighthood was in flower and Arabian
culture was the admiration of the world.
Fortunately we shall have to put no constraint upon
ourselves to make the welcome manifest and notable.
The more that come, the merrier will the welcome be.
We can never be weary of entertaining our kinsmen
of the East and showing them how far California sur
passes anything they ever saw at home. In shorty we
have a double satisfaction in greeting all who came;
first, that of receiving visitors whom we -are pleased
to meet, and second, that of exhibiting the beauties
and the splendors of our matchless State.
California has long been the "excursion State" of
the Union and San Francisco is rapidly becoming the
chief summer convention city. Last season we had
a host of visitors within our gates, and this year will
see our streets thronged by a host more numerous
still. By reason of the prestige we have acquired in
that, way a new glory has come to us and a new re
sponsibility rests upon us. From this tirne >on we
shall doubtless have every summer a great throng of
visitors from all parts of the Union, and it will be
our duty to maintain undiminished the repute of hos
pitality that has come down to us from the days of the
pioneers.
FOR the Mystic Shriners San Francisco has a
welcome ardent as the Orient and lavish as the
West. -To reach us many of them have liter
ally "crossed the hot sands." â¢ They will find this
city a true midsummer oasis, the like of which exists
nowhere else on the globe.

again Miller scored, taking the fifth goal

518 the following are the nationalities:

mans 27, Scandinavians 12, Spanish-Amer

iners, 26 farmers, 22 farm laborers, 24 mis
cellaneous not stated, 10 laborers, 9 en
gineers, 7 miners, 6 carpenters, 4 hotel

and children. Seven Immigrants were de

The annual entertainment given by the
nurses of the Homeopathic Sanatorium
was held last night at the nurses' cot
tage on Central avenue. There were none
but the nurses and their immediate
'rh'e deception room of the cottage had
been made gay with numerous beautiful
floral gifts sent the young ladles by many
who had been under their care. Parlor
games had been arranged and, of course,
there was among the amusements offered
the now famous ping pong, for which
there were dainty prizes. One of the most
amusing of the games, however, was the
improvised "fish-pond," which contained
all manner of ridiculous articles
The young ladies were armed with small
fishing rods and as each one would bring
up from the water a dilapidated shoe or
an old straw hat, a toy kitten and other
things equally ludicrous there would be
an outburst of hearty laughter. Ping
pon? was voted out of service after the
amusement derived from the "fish-pond"
game.
During the evening there was singing
and Instrumental music.
Those of the nursing staff who partici
pated in the entertainment were Misses
Blanch Minaker, Lily Newell, Lester
Hickey, Irene Hopps, Janet Hopps, Ruth
Hopps, Francis Emerline Southern, Leila
Forschler, Elizabeth Ham, Eleanor Bro
phy. Pearl Wycoff, Ellen Kelly, Christine
Minugh, Clara Hoppin, Eugenia Yarring
ton. Edith Cole, Margaret Powell, Mrs.
McVlcker and Miss Etna B. Chattin,
superintendent of the sanatorium.

Sell Liquor Without License.
August Koehnke, Tony Phillips, Anton
Meyer and Louis P. Matkin appeared be
fore Judge Cabaniss yesterday on a
charge of selling liquor without a 'license
and the cases were continued till Thurs
day. They are four of the saloon-keepers
whose licenses were not renewed recent
ly by the Police Commissioners owing to
the proximity of their saloons to the Pre-
Eidio. Chief Wittman learned that liquor
was being sold in these places and de
tailed officers in plain clothes to visit them
with the result that warrants were sworn
out for their arrest.
A letter from Henry Miller, United
States Consul at Newchwang, China, to
a prominent commercial firm of this city
says, in response to inquiries ln regard to
truae possibilities ln Mongolia, that there
is a great demand for beer, wines, liquors,
meats, canned fruits, general groceries,
bocts and shoes, candles, flour, milk, soap,
candles, cotton and woolen goods. It fs
stated that business must be conducted in
the Russian language and that only such
gocd s can be sold as may please the Rus
sians.

All arrangements have been concluded
for GÂ«orge Gardner to go to Chicago and
box -Philadelphia" Jack O'Brien. Greg
gains telegraphed for tickets, and as soon
as they arrive he* will depart with his
protege for the scene of future hostilities.
Gardner and O'Brien will weigh in at 162
pounds. Gardner is doing light work, pre
paratory for his bout.
Manager Alec Greggains stated yester
day that this morning he would sign a
contract for the erection of a building at
Valencia and Fourteenth streets, in wnich
to bring off the championship' battle be
tween itobert Fitzsimmons and James J.
Jeffries. He said the contract would be
let to William Faber, the carpenter who
has always looked after Woodward's Pa
vilion. The new structure svill be imme
diately opposite this pavilion. It will be
a wooden structure and large enough :o
accommodate 15,000 people. Greggains
thinks the cost of this building will be far
less than the price which the people who
control Mechanics* Pavilion wish to
charge for its rentaLf He further stated
that seats in the gallery will be sold for
$5 each, and the chairs close to the ring
will be disposed of for prices that are
higher, but reasonable.

Breeds Dandruff, Which. Causes Fall-

Professor Unna, Hamburg, Germany,
European authority on skin diseases, says
that dandruff is as contagious as any oth-
er malevolent disease, and that one com-
mon source of the spread of dandruff is
the use of the same hair brush by differ-
ent persons. The way to avoid catching
dandruff or any other disease from an-
other's brush is to Insist on the use of
Newbro's Herpiclde. It not only kills the
dandruff germ, but it is also an antiseptic
that will prevent, the catching of any dis-
ease whatever through contagion of an-
other's brush.

A compromise was effected yesterday in
the contest over the estate of the late
John J. Shaughnessy, the well-known
commercial traveler, who died recently.
According to the terms of the compromise
Anna Shaughnessy, who claims to be we
widow of the drummer, receives 45 per
cent of his estate and his sister, Mrs.
Margaret Hobson, receives 55 per cent.
The estate is estimated to be wortn $20,000.
Rival Claimants Compromise.
The estate of the late Kachael Salz,
widow of the late J. Salz of the firm of
Kullmann, Salz & Co., tanners, has
been appraised at 5172,720. The re
port of the - appraisers, which ' was
filed yesterday, shows thafe her. estate
consists of realty in Alameda and
Stockton worth $18,500. stock in country
banks worth $4700, $14,370 cash and an In
terest in the firm, of Kullmann, Salz &
Co. worth $135,000.
Her Estate Is Valuable.

The British ship Nivelle. which arrived yes
terday 132 days from Hamburg, failed to stop
In the quarantine grounds and was well up
the harbor when spied by "Tommy" Crowley,
the well known boatman. Crowley had busi
ness with Captain McMillan and without wait
ing for the quarantine- officials to. pass on her
health conditions he ran alongside the llme
juicer and talked with the skipper. Finding
she had not been passed by the doctor Crowley
declined to go aboard and was gasolinlng for
home when the quarantine tug . overhauled
him.
"Come aboard," said Dr. Cummings of the
Marine Hospital service.

The Pacific Mail Company's steamship City
of Sydney sailed for Panama and way ports.

more than seventy passengers. Of these fifty

THE Oceanic Steamship Company's
liner Australia sailed yesterday
for Tahiti. She. carried passen
gers and took a large cargo of
general merchandise. Among the
passengers was Miss E. G. Howard,
daughter of Captain H. Z. H*ward, ma
rine superintendent of the Oceanic Steam
ship Company. Accompanying Miss How
arG was Mrs. John L. Prior, wife of the
well-known sailmaker. ;
Suits for divorce were filed yesterday by
Ella Pepperdine against Silas W. Pepper
dine for failure to provide, Harry Hatton
against Emma Hatton for desertion and.
Josephine Fredricks against C. J. Fred
ricks for failure to provide.
Divorces were granted to Lulu Keep
Waldon from Ellas W. Waldon for
neglect, Nora Whitaker from F. M. Whit
aker for desertion, J. W. Blundell from
Agnes B. Blundell for desertion and Ura
nia Quaid from D. J. Quaid for neglect.
Irwin R, Colbrandt has brought a suit
for divorce against Ada H. Colbrandt for
desertion. He alleges that she left him
last May. They were married in March,
1899, and have one child, who has been in
the custody of Mrs. Rose Bernard for the
last year.
-"I will continue this matter unui to
morrow at noon, when I shall expect you
to have proof, in the shape of the books
of the railroad company, that your client
is speaking the truth."
"Well, he has not shown it." said Ker
rigan. "The testimony in the divorce pro- )
ceedings last October showed that . he
treated his wife with gross cruelty, and
his conduct since then has not placed him
in the favor of this court. Me has not
paid his wife a cent of alimony since last
March, "and I want to say right here that
1 am not at all disposed to treat him with
any leniency.
Eagan offers as an excuse for his action
that he has been on a vacation and has
not been in receipt of his usual salary,
but Judge Kerrigan intimated that he did
not place much credence in the statement.
"This man is willing to support his fam
ily," vouchsafed Eagan's attorney as _is
client left the witness stand.
/
Thomas' B. Eagan, a foreman of con
struction of the United Railroads, learned
yesterday in Judge Kerrigan's court that
the practice of refusing to obey a court
order would lead him into difficulties and
place him in an unenviable light before
the world." He appeared before Judge
Kerrigan yesterday on an order to show
cause why he should not be punished for
contempt of court for allowing his di
vorced wife and seven children to. remain
in want when he had been ordered by the
court to contribute $65 a month for their
support. This is Eagan's third appear
ance before Judge Kerrigan on orders to
show cause, and the court informed him
in very plain language that his conduct
was despicable in the extreme.

Califondans in New York.
NEW YORK, June 9.â The following
Calif ornians are In New York:
Detectives Dinan and Wren have beÂ«n
working Bteadily in their efforts to locate
the accomplices of William White, fore
man In the liquor department of Olson
& Co., grocers, 715 Market street, who
was arrested last Friday on two charges
of grand larceny. They have succeeded
and yesterday morning secured warrants
from Judge Cabaniss for the arrest of
Owen Heffernan and "John DoÂ«" John
ston. Johnston carted the stolen liquor
away from the store and Heffernan sold
It to saloon-keepers and others In the
"tenderloin." Johnston used to bÂ© train
er for George Green, the pugilist.
White appeared before Judge Cabaniss
yesterday on the two charges of grand
larceny. He -was Instructed and arraigned
and the cases .were continued till to-mor
row for the preliminary hearing. It Is
estimated that during the last month
White with the aid of his two accom
plices has stolen between $2000 and 13000
worth of the best brands of liquor.

Irving M. Scott said last night that all
he knew of the shipbuilders' combine was
gleaned from the columns of The Call. He
denied that the Union Iron Works had
entered Into such a combine.
L.OS ANGELES, June 9.âH. E. Huntlnj
ton Is In New York. Superintendent Ran
dolph of his lines denies all knowledge of
Huntlngton's shipbuilding Interests ex
cept the fact that he owns the shipyards
at Newport News, Va. ; '<;
Douglas G. Moore, who is connected
with the Crescent Iron Worka, also con
firmed the report that the deal had been
completed, and that an announcement
would be made to-day. He said: "An op
tion has been given to the plant of the
Crescent Iron Works, and other options
are held on plants scattered along the At
lantic seaboard, and at least two plants on
the Pacific are to be absorbed."
Lewis Nikon, who has been most active
In bringing about the combination, again
refused to-day to discuss hi3 plans, but
one who has been associated with him
during the last month in the work ot
forming the new corporation said: "You
may say that the deal is completed. The
only reasons why formal announcement
was not made to-day Is that the more Im
portant details occupied the time until It
was too late to properly frame what la to
be said to the public. ' That will be done
to-morrow. The securities have been
written and one of the strongest banking
Institutions in the city will accept sub
scriptions. The men who have pledged
their credit to the enterprise guarantee ita
success."
NEW YORK, June 9.â All but the merest
formalities of the plan to combine several
of the big shipbuilding yards ot the coun
try under one management were com
pleted to-day. A lew papers remain yet
to be signed, and for this purpose a meet
ing of those most interested win be held
to-morrow morning:, alter whicn formal
announcement will t>e maue and the secu
rities of the new corporation will be offer
ed to the public. In spite of the extreme
reticence of the men who are promoting
the formation of the combination, It was*
learned to-day that the plans contemplate
a company which will turn out any Kind
of vessel, from a steamship of small di
mensions to a battleship weighted down
with the heaviest of armor. Iron and
steel works are being negotiated for, and
It is understood that several extensive
plants have been purchased, among them
the Crescent Iron Works at Ellzabethport,
New Jersey.

ADVANCE GUABD WINS
THE STANDARD STAKES
The Colonial Secretary. Joseph Cham
berlain, answering a question regarding
the agitation for the suspension of the
constitution of Cape Colony, said he had
received a petition from forty-two mem
bers of the Parliament of Cape' Colony
in favor of Its suspension, and had await
ed the observations of the cape ministry
on the subject. An act . of the imperial
Parliament, he added, was required before
the constitution could be suspended.
BLOEMFONTEIN, Orange River Col
ony, June 9.â A strong contrast to the
feeling between Boer and Briton Is the
bitterness existing between the burghers
who are surrendering under the peace
terms and' those Boers who surrendered
during the campaign. When the Boer
leaders came to Bloemfonteln, previous to
the conclusion of peace, they curtly re
fused to shake hands with those who had
previously surrendered. : The general be
lief is that relations between these two
classes of Boers. will be embittered for a
lone time.
WASHINGTON, June 9.â President
Roosevelt had among his callers to-day
General J. M. Bell, who was accompanied
by Charles E. Prince, a prominent resi
dent of Edinburgh Scotland. In conver
sation . the President * expressed to Mr.
Prince his gratification at the ending of
the war in South Africa, and Indulged the
hope that the British and Boers would
amalgamate rapidly and s,ettle down to
their accustomed dally pursuits.
LONDON, June 9.â The suggestion that
Lord Kitchener, with the connivance of
the Government, entered into a* secret
compact with the Boers to induce them
to surrender, was denied by the Govern
ment leader, A. J. Balfour, in the House
cf Commons this afternoon.

president \ of the Chamber of Commerce

shire Hotel last evening. The
ceremony was performed by Rev. George
C. Adams, pastor of the First Congrega
tional Church, of which the groom Is a
member. W. E. F. Deal, the well-known
attorney, gave the bride. Into the keeping
of the groom, iThe floral decorations were
artistic and novel in many respects. It
was a pink and white wedding. The bridal
bower in one corner of the drawing-room
was of pink net and many clusters of
gypsy pillow tied with ribbons of white
and leaf green. Odd baskets of ferns
were also interspersed. The mantel was
banked in with Bride roses, over which
was an immense white wreath tied with ,
pink ribbons. An aisle leading to the
bower was of pink and white sweet peas.
The halls were done in ferns and potted
palms. Grape vines were effectively ar
ranged in the banquet room and the ta
bles were elaborately decorated with yel-
I low coreopsis and maidenhair ferns.
The bride was beautiful and charming
in a shell pink chiffon, the elegance and
simplicity of which suited her face and
splendid figure. The gown was a close
fitting affair of solidly shirred tucks. The
clinging skirt flared about the feet in
three graduated flounces, edged with
three broad tucks. ' The shirred bodice
had a transparent yoke of tucked chiffon
and a bertha and collar of real Duchess
lace. The sleeves were of shirred tucks
to the elbow, with a flowing effect below,
caught in at the hand with shirred chif
fon. A pink aigrette. completed the toi
lette.
The nuptials were followed by a recep
tion and' supper. The bride is an Eastern
weman of liberal education and refine-,
ment,- who has lived irt Los Angeles and
this city for several years. Many of her
Los Angeles friends came up for the wed
ding. . ,
Mr. Foulks is an old-time resident of
this city, having graduated from the
Boys' High School and the University of
California, and has been practicing law
here for several years past. Mr. Foulks,
while in college, took high military hon
ors, was in track athletics and
was Berkeley's first football captain in
the intercollegiate games. He is at pres
ent the head of the Commercial Evening
School. He is a member of the Olympic
Club and of the San Francisco Flycast
ing Club.
Mr. and Mrs. Foulks left for an extend
ed, trip throughout the East.' They will go
by the Ogden route and return by the
Canadian Pacific. Upon their return they
will reside in this city, where a new home
awaits them.

Mr. Newman was rather worried over
the strenuous "turn the row over posses
sion of the saloon took last nisht. He
thinks it will cause a vast falling off of
business and permanently injure the rep
utation of. the saloon. â¢
The row over the possession of the
Richelieu saloon, which has been In pro
gress between Herbert Choynski % and
Charles Newman for over a week, came
near ending in a serious shooting affray
last night. Newman, accompanied by his
brother, George Baker, his attorney. Mose
Getz and a barkeeper, entered the saloon
about 8 o'clock, just as Choynskt and his
barkeepers and several fighting men were
assuming possession of the- place after
Choynski had raised an attachment that
had been issued against the quarreling
firm earlier in the day. When the Deputy
Sheriff who was in charge opened the
doors of the saloon, which had been closed
since 3 o'clock in the afternoon, Choynski
and his supporters stepped Into the
saloon, closely followed by the Newman
contingent. Choynski ordered Newman
out of the place. Choynskl's three bar
tenders were by this time behind the bar
and all three of them pulled revolvers and
pointed the weapons at Newman and his
party, and while the men behind the bar
kept the opposing party Â«at bay Choynski
ordered Newman and his friends to leave
the place immediately. They retired
quietly to the street, leaving Choynski
and his armed bartenders in possession.
The attachment issued yesterday was
sworn out by a collector warned Isaacs
on behalf of William Wolf, the wholesale
liquor dealer. After the Sheriff's office
closed last night Choynski made a flank
movement and raised the attachment .by
placing $2000 in cash in the hands of Sher
iff Laekmann. Â¦ â¢ â¢

Compton avenue is a part of the route
covered by the famous "freight fran
chise," which aroused discussion several
months ago, and is the urban connection
for the Long Beach electric road, now
being built by the Pacific Electric Rail
way Company. The franchise was granted
to the company with a freight carrying
privilege attached, and, after being sold
to the Pacific Electric Company, was ve
toed by Mayor Snyder. Suit was brought
by the company to compel the city to al
low the work of construction to begin
â¢under the terms of the franchise claimed
by the company. The case is now pending
in the courts and the company officials
claim that to-day's move was made neces
sary by the provision of the disputed
franchise which required that the con
struction should be begun within four
months after the franchise was granted
The time limit will expire- on June 13.
LOS AXGELES. June 9. â Eight hundred
men were put at work this, morning by
the Pacific Electric Railway Company,
laying track on Compton avenue, in the
southeastern part of this city. As soon
as the property owners became aware of
the move of the company to gain posses
sion of the street, they appealed to the
city authorities and a squad of policemen
was sent to the scene with instructions
to stop the work. Before this was accom
plished the construction gang had succeed
ed in laying a quarter of a mile of broad
gauge track in the record time of less
than three hours.

OXNARD, June 9.â Four Mexicans ' en
gaged in mortal combat here at 12:30
o'clock this morning. Three of them are
now in the County Hospital. The county
surgeon says that two men cannot live
and the third has only a bare chance. The
fcurth is in the County Jail In Ventura.
The participants in the affray used re
volvers and knives. The wounded are:
Santiago Fernandez,- shot In the jaw
with a pistol, the bullet lodging in his
head.
Eduardo Acosta, shot in the back, bul
let taken from the breast.
Miguel Fabiola, suffering from' knife
wounds in the back.
The imprisoned Mexican Is Joe Guitano.
The affray occurred In the Mexican set
tlement. Within a few minutes : the en
tire neighborhood was aroused. When
omcers arrived on the battle ground throe
cambatants were unable to continue the
fight or offer resistance. Neighbors cap
tured Guitano without much trouble, as
he was exhausted.'
The trouble originated with a family
affair. Some weeks ago the wife of Fer
nandez obtained a divorce. She is Gui
tano's mother.
The two men were quarreling when
Acosta and Fabiola attempted to inter
fere. Acosta and Guitano have long been
enemies, owing . to attentions alleged to
have been paid by the former to Guitano's
wife. The quarrel soon became a melee,
in which all four men were participants.
According to the reports, Fernandez
stabbed Fabiola and Guitano did the*
shooting, wounding Fernandez and
Acosta.
"But," said the banker, "there have
been certain business transactions
through this bank whereby I know the
report to be true."
Something definite will be known soon.
as Hooker's option will expire about
July L .i
One thing that substantiates the belief
that it means a new sugar factory is the
fact that a few days ago S. H. Brown, a
furniture dealer of this place, was In Los
Angeles, and in conversation with a
friend, who is connected with a bank in
that city, the latter made the remark that
Oxnard was to have another sugar fac
tory. To this Brown replied that he did
not believe there was anything in the re
port.
Peile was practically the only one who
krew of this transaction, and he kept It
Quiet until a few days ago, when .he re
ceived a letter from Hooker, dated Phila
delphia. This letter simply announced
that Hooker's project was going through
all right and that he would be heard from
Boon.
There arrived in Oxnard about May 1 a
gentleman who kept his mission and even
nis name a secret. He hired a livery rig
and took a lung drive about- the valley,
making inquiries regarding the water sup
piy, etc. Several days after he left
Cniirles S. Jfeile. a local real estate agent,
received a letter from H. C. Hooker,
dated Los Angeles, asking for terms upon
certain pieces of land in the vicinity of
Oxnard. Feile mailed the requested in
formation and soon afterward received a
telegram stating that Hooker was coming
to Oxrard and desired to have Peile meet
him at the station with a carriage. This
was done, and Hooker requested that he
be taken to see some land east of town.
He would give no information, except
that the land should be near the railroad,
have plenty of water and be so situated
as to give a direct fall to the ocean.
The&e requirements were met in a tract
of 126 acres three miles east of Oxnard,
belonging to John G. HilL Hooker asked
fcr a sixty-day option on this property,
which was given him, and he deposited
money to bind the bargain. As it was then
nearly train time, Hooker drove to the
etation. Peile was naturally anxious to
know what it all meant and asked to be
enlightened. Hooker's reply was:
"If any one asks you, just tell them, you
don't know."
With this the gentleman left for Los
Angeles.
OXNARD, June 9.â H. C. Hooker of
Philadelphia has obtained a sixty-day op
tion on 136 acres of land three miles east
of Oxnard, and If reports are true this
vicinity is soon to have another immense
sugar factory. What is puzzling the pop
ulace is the mystery connected with the
negotiations, which yield no information
as to what interests Hooker represents.

Potatoes, Onions and Vegetables.
Receipts of new Potatoes were light, but the
market showed little improvement, . as most of
the offerings were poor. A few lots of the
best stock sold as' high as 2c per pound. Old
Potatoes were unchanged. The Oregon steamer
brought down 25G0 sacks.
Supplies of Onions continued plentiful, and
prices were "unchanged.
Tomatoes from Mississippi continued to sell
readily at $2 25@2 50 per crate. There were a
few crates received from Los Angeles, and the
Panama steamer brought up a small conslgn
.ment from Mexico. Summer Squash and Cu
cumbers from Marysville were in liberal re
ceipt and prices had a wider range. Receipts
of other vegetables were moderate and the mar
ket-cleaned up well.

Butter, Cheese and Eggs. \ '
The : week opened with Â¦ large â¢ receipts cf
Butter, as will be seen. . Prices showed no.
change, but the feeling was unquestionably
easy, with more- sellers than buyers. The pro-'
posed introduction of oleomargarine Into the
California market by a large Chicago concern
is agitating the trade, especially as a local
factory proposes to follow suit. Dealers say
that contractors for lumber, Â¦ railroad and fish
ing camps are large buyers of oleomargarine
whenever It is on the market, and that a cer
tain class of bakers also use it freely, so that
the imitation product will be very apt to pro
duce a depressing effect on packed Butter later
on In the season. This has a tendency to
check the current packing of fresh Butter and
is an additional element of weakness. An ac
count of the action â¢ of Â¦ the Dairy Exchange in
the matter yesterday appears in the local
columns.
. Further changes In Cheese will be noted. Old
is now in such meager supply that few if any
dealers are quoting it. Supplies of new, while
sufficient for current needs, are not as ex
cessive as they have been. '
Ranch Eggs are quoted higher by "most
houses, though receipts showed a marked .in
crease yesterday. Spot stocks of Eggs are ap
parently lighter and the feeling is, rather firm
than otherwise. . *
Receipts were 79,100 pounds, 276 cubes and
4 kegs of Butter, 1362 cases, of - Eggs, -400
cases of Eastern Eggs, 5600 pounds of Califor
nia Cheese and pounds of Oregon Cheese.

NEW YORK, June 9.â The dullness of the
tradine in stocks was unrelieved to-day, but
the inertia of prices was not quite so absolute.
There was a scattering distribution of buying
orders during the early trading which lifted
prices slightly. The demand subsequently dlel
away, but the higher level of prices was pretty
well maintained during the rest of the day.
News of weather conditions favorable to ail
of the crops seemed to be the cause of the
buying, which was most prominent in Atchi
son. That Mock and St. Paul were moved up
a point. Vasrue rumors were current all day
of a coming settlement of the coal strike and
operators in stocks DrofeHsed satisfaction over
the small effect of the soft coal miners' strike,
ine public statement made on behalf of the
anthracite operators declaring themselves sat
isfied with the success attained in preventing
damage to the mines by flooding was also cited
by buyers of stocks. The presence to-day of
the United States Commissioner of Labor anl
the interest in the strike thus manifested on
oenalf of the President were variously re
garded. The question of statutory power of
the President to acti\ely Intervene, however,
seemed to afford most satisfaction to those in
terested In stocks. , Reading's gain reached a
point. The rise of 1% in Hocking Valley was
apparently due to the fact that the labor
trouble. has .not affected Its territory. Sugar
had the benefit of a rise in the price of renned
product and of hopes of an agreement on

WEATHER AND CROP CONDITIONS.
Chlcoâ Barley good; wheat good.
Hollister â Hay baling begun; crop yielding
better than expected. :
Hanford â Grain being harvested; peaches de
veloping rapidly; apricots in market.
Palermoâ Shipment apricots by express. â¢
Newman â grain ripening rapidly.
Willows â Wheat fine; loss by rain ten days
ago overestimated.
Ventura â Foggy weather continues,! making
prospects better for summer crops.
Colusa â Fruit and grain crops are in good
condition.
San Jose â Grain and hay crop excellent;
fruit crop coming on fast; big crop.
Livermore â Barley and wheat doing well;
heavy crop cherries.
Napa â All crops doing well.
Cioverdale â All indications of an abundant
fruit crop.
Santa Rosa â Fruit and grain doing well.
Santa Maria â Grain being headed; good aver
age quality; sugar beets doing well.
"An unusually Â¦warm spell at the beginning
of the week, during which the maximum tem
perature was between 80 degrees and 90 de
grees at different places, was followed by a
steady decrease in temperature, and on the
23th there were cold showers, with hall
mingled. On the morning of the 30th there was
light frost in some localities in the eastern
counties. The hail did some damage to young
firuit. and the frost slightly injured tender
Vegetables. The dry tpell with the three warm
days warmed up the soil so that the crops hav*"
rrown well, notwithstanding the cool weather
of the greater part of the past week.
"Grass, clover, alfalfa and grain of all sorts
have flourished. Considerable alfalfa Is now
about ready for cutting; a small amount has
been cut. Wheat, both spring and winter. Is
in very satisfactory condition. The reports
from the Palouse country are extremely favor
able. Barley in Columbia County and oats In
Ekagit County are above the average. Hops
are making good progress. Early potatoes are
In blossom and looking splendid; late ones are
growing nicely, except on very wet soils. Sugar
beets are in satisfactory condition.
"Good reports come from the peach sections,
east of the mountains, and the outlook for ap
ples is. on the whole, favorable. But cherries,
prunes and pears have dropped off eo that only
a small crop is left. Strawberries are ripening
and .with favorable weather for the next two
weeks the crop will be large." .

CHICAGO, June 9.â Wheat was depressed by
the fine weather more . than by anything else/
For several days the' short interest had been
much worried over the prospects of damage to
winter wheat by a wet ; harvest. In ' conse
quence there .'was , considerable", covering .and
the bulls bought recently to a fair extent. To
day all this was changed when 'the weather
map showed clear weather prevailing general
ly.. The Southwestern markets were weak at '
the start, St. Louis being down and selling
here on the weather. : The local crowd was very
bearish at the start and July opened %@%c to
%c down at 71%c to 71%@71%c, ; and soon
tumbled to 71 % c. The cessation of a demand
from shorts eliminated the otherwise bullish in
fluences that might have been expected from
a decrease of 2,640,000 bushels in the on-passage
stuff, and of 2,613,000 bushels in the! visible
supply. Even some talk of exporting new stuff
and a fair show of strength at-New York on
the French demand at weakened prices had no
effect. Wheat was very heavy and seemed un
able to help itself, ana as no support came to
its aid, the close was weak, July l@l%c lower
at 71%@71%c.
â¢ Corn opened 'off sharply on favorable weather
for cultivation and on free offerings. ! There
was, . however, active buying of July Â¦: by the
big bull party that is supposed, to be in con-,
trol of that option. July closed weak, %@%c
down at <>2%<8>62%. :
Oats broke with the other grains and ruled
weak. July closed weak, %@%c down at
38%c. Â¦â¢'..
Â¦ Provisions did not Â¦ feel the grain influence
and were pushed up to f higher prices. ' Hogs
were higher,, there was a. fair demand by shorts
and little stuff was offered by the packers who
control all the holdings. July pork closed 7%c
up, lard closed 6@7%c up and ribs 20c higher.

Lemons were firmer under a steady demand
and light supplies. Supplies of fancy Oranges
were limited and , top prices were easily ob
tained for offerings. At the auction 2 cars were
sold as follows: Navels, $3 73 for 2S boxes of
fancy and $2 25 for choice; choice Valencias.
$1 50@2 75; Seedlings. $2 15 for fancy and
$1@1 75 for choice. Some choice Lemons were
auctioned also and brought from $ 1 SO to $1 70
per box. . : â¢ â¢
Three more crates of Nutmeg Melons came
in from Yuraa. They contained from 12 to 15
Melons each and were sold at the uniform
price of $5 per crate.
Receipts of new green fruits continue light
and prices stand about the same.
Apricots were offering freely and common
stock was a little lower. Fancy Seedlings in
small boxes sold readily at full figures.
Cherries showed little change and the mar
ket-was well cleaned up at the end of the
day. The demand for loose stock was par
ticularly brisk and It was not difficult to ob
tain the top quotation for fancy Black. The
canners bought common white at 2@3c and
secured some Royal Anne at 5%c.
and prices were generally lower. As usual some
poor Strawberries were received and were eold
below the : quotations. Strawberries in . crates
from Florin were in good condition and sold
promptly. Other' Berries in crates were most
ly poor and sold slowly. Prices on Currants
had a | wider range, as some of the receipts
came to hand overripe and leaking.

Pollards Join Band of Mercy.
Mrs. Charles Holbrook, wife of the Sec
retary of the Society for the Prevention
of Cruelty to Children, formed the forty
two members of the Pollard Juvenile
Opera Company Into a band of mercy at
tl'e Windsor Hotel yesterday. â¢ The chil
dren all promised to prevent cruelty to
animals so far as shall lie in their power
.and to try to found other similar bands
v.hen they shall return to Australia.

COMMERCIAL NEWS
A short time ago the Secretary of the
Treasury inaugurated- a new system of
handling samples of the teas shipped
through this port from the Orient. Hith
erto it had been the practice for the tea
examiner to select the samples out of the
packages and have them sent to the Ap
praiser's store. Under the new regulation
the importer or consignee is obliged to
select the samples.: This entails consid
erable labor and time, and in consequence
all the tea firms in the city have pro
tested against the new order of things,
and have prepared a petition requesting
the Secretary of the .Treasury to restore
the old regulation. Collector Stratton and
Appraiser Dare have recommended a re
turn to the old system.
Tea Importers Dissatisfied.
